Shalyapin\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_author_institution\" content=\"Departamento de F\u00edsica Moderna, Universidad de Cantabria, Avda. de Los Castros s/n, 39005 Santander, Spain\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_author_institution\" content=\"Institute for Radiophysics and Electronics, National Academy of Sciences of Ukraine, 12 Proskura St., 61085 Kharkov, Ukraine\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_title\" content=\"Gravitational lens system SDSS J1339+1310: microlensing factory and time delay\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_online_date\" content=\"2016/12/02\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_publication_date\" content=\"2016/12/01\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_volume\" content=\"596\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_firstpage\" content=\"A77\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_doi\" content=\"10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_bibcode\" content=\"2016A%26A...596A..77G\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_abstract_html_url\" content=\"https://www.aanda.org/articles/aa/abs/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_fulltext_html_url\" content=\"https://www.aanda.org/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_pdf_url\" content=\"https://www.aanda.org/articles/aa/pdf/2016/12/aa28790-16.pdf\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_issn\" content=\"0004-6361\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_issn\" content=\"1432-0746\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_language\" content=\"en\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_keyword\" content=\"gravitational lensing: strong\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_keyword\" content=\"gravitational lensing: micro\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_keyword\" content=\"quasars: individual: SDSS J1339+1310\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_keyword\" content=\"cosmological parameters\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_article_type\" content=\"Research Article\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_abstract\" lang=\"en\" content=\"We spectroscopically re-observed the gravitational lens system SDSS J1339+1310 using OSIRIS on the GTC. We also monitored the &lt;i&gt;r&lt;i/&gt;-band variability of the two quasar images (A and B) with the LT over 143 epochs in the period 2009\u20132016. These new data in both the wavelength and time domains have confirmed that the system is an unusual microlensing factory. The C\u2009iv emission line is remarkably microlensed, since the microlensing magnification of B relative to that for A, &lt;i&gt;\u03bc&lt;i/&gt;&lt;sub&gt;BA&lt;sub/&gt;, reaches a value of 1.4 (~0.4 mag) for its core. Moreover, the B image shows a red wing enhancement of C\u2009iv flux (relative to A), and &lt;i&gt;\u03bc&lt;i/&gt;&lt;sub&gt;BA&lt;sub/&gt; = 2 (0.75 mag) for the C\u2009iv broad-line emission. Regarding the nuclear continuum, we find a chromatic behaviour of &lt;i&gt;\u03bc&lt;i/&gt;&lt;sub&gt;BA&lt;sub/&gt;, which roughly varies from ~5 (1.75 mag) at 7000 \u00c5 to ~6 (1.95 mag) at 4000 \u00c5. We also detect significant microlensing variability in the &lt;i&gt;r&lt;i/&gt; band, and this includes a number of microlensing events on timescales of 50\u2013100 d. Fortunately, the presence of an intrinsic 0.7 mag dip in the light curves of A and B, permitted us to measure the time delay between both quasar images. This delay is \u0394&lt;i&gt;t&lt;i/&gt;&lt;sub&gt;AB&lt;sub/&gt; = 47&lt;sup&gt;+5&lt;sup/&gt;&lt;sub&gt;-6&lt;sub/&gt; d (1&lt;i&gt;\u03c3&lt;i/&gt; confidence interval; A is leading), in good agreement with predictions of lens models.\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_funding_source\" content=\"citation_funder=Spanish Department of Research, Development and Innovation; citation_grant_number=AYA2013-47744-C3-2-P;\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_funding_source\" content=\"citation_funder=Universidad de Cantabria; citation_funder_id=http://dx.doi.org/10.13039/501100006365;\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_reference\" content=\"citation_author=C. XHTML 1.0 Transitional//EN\">\n\n<div class=\"menu\" id=\"bloc\"><ul>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#top_full\">Top</a></li>\n<li><a Microlensing in ...</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S7\">4. Light curves in the <i>r</i> band</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S8\" title=\"Time delay and r-band microlensing variability\">5. Time delay ...</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S12\">6. Conclusions</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#ack\">Acknowledgments</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#ref\">References</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#app\">Appendix A:</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#tabs\">List of tables</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#figs\">List of figures</a></li>\n</ul></div>\n<div id=\"contenu\">\n<a name=\"top_full\"></a><div id=\"head\">A&amp;A 596, A77 (2016)<h2 class=\"title\">Gravitational lens system SDSS J1339+1310: microlensing factory and time delay<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN1\">&#8902;</a></sup>\n</h2>\n<div class=\"article-authors\"><p class=\"bold\"><span id=\"aa28790-16-author-1\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790&amp;n=1\" class=\"author\">L. J. Goicoechea</span><sup>1</sup> and <span id=\"aa28790-16-author-2\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790&amp;n=2\" class=\"author\">V. N. Shalyapin</span><sup>1</sup><sup>,2</sup></p></div>\n<p class=\"aff\"><sup>1</sup> Departamento de F&#237;sica Moderna, Universidad de Cantabria, Avda. de Los Castros s/n,  39005   Santander,  Spain \n<br>\n            e-mail: goicol@unican.es\n            \n          <br><sup>2</sup> Institute for Radiophysics and Electronics, National Academy of Sciences of Ukraine, 12 Proskura St.,  61085   Kharkov,  Ukraine \n<br>\n            e-mail: vshal@ukr.net\n            \n          <br></p>\n<p class=\"history\"><span class=\"bold\">Received: </span>25 April 2016<br><span class=\"bold\">Accepted: </span>8 September 2016</p>\n<p class=\"bold\"><a name=\"abs\"></a>Abstract</p>\n        <p>We spectroscopically re-observed the gravitational lens system SDSS J1339+1310 using OSIRIS on the GTC. We also monitored the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band variability of the two quasar images (A and B) with the LT over 143 epochs in the period 2009&#8211;2016. These new data in both the wavelength and time domains have confirmed that the system is an unusual microlensing factory. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission line is remarkably microlensed, since the microlensing magnification of B relative to that for A, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub></span>, reaches a value of 1.4 (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.4 mag) for its core. Moreover, the B image shows a red wing enhancement of C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>flux (relative to A), and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub></span> = 2 (0.75 mag) for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>broad-line emission. Regarding the nuclear continuum, we find a chromatic behaviour of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub></span>, which roughly varies from <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5 (1.75 mag) at 7000 &#197; to <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>6 (1.95 mag) at 4000 &#197;. We also detect significant microlensing variability in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band, and this includes a number of microlensing events on timescales of 50&#8211;100 d. Fortunately, the presence of an intrinsic 0.7 mag dip in the light curves of A and B, permitted us to measure the time delay between both quasar images. This delay is <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub></span> = 47<sup>+5</sup><sub>-6</sub> d (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence interval; A is leading), in good agreement with predictions of lens models.</p>\n      <div class=\"kword\"><p><span class=\"bold\">Key words: </span>gravitational lensing: strong / gravitational lensing: micro / quasars: individual: SDSS J1339+1310 / cosmological parameters</p></div>\n<div class=\"note\">\n<hr width=\"30%\" align=\"left\">\n<div>\n<a name=\"FN1\"></a><sup>&#8902;</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">Tables 1&#8211;3 are only available at the CDS via anonymous ftp to <a href=\"http://cdsarc.u-strasbg.fr\" target=\"_blank\">cdsarc.u-strasbg.fr</a> (<a href=\"http://130.79.128.5\" target=\"_blank\">130.79.128.5</a>) or via <a href=\"http://cdsarc.u-strasbg.fr/viz-bin/qcat?J/A+A/596/A77\" target=\"_blank\">http://cdsarc.u-strasbg.fr/viz-bin/qcat?J/A+A/596/A77</a></p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i>&#169; ESO, 2016</i></p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S1\"></a>1. Introduction</h2>\n<p>If there is a massive galaxy between a distant quasar and the Earth, the quasar is seen as a multiple system consisting of several images (e.g. <a name=\"InR82\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R82\">Schneider et al. 1992</a>). This gravitationally lensed quasar may also suffer microlensing effects by stellar mass objects in the lensing galaxy. Quasar microlensing was firstly detected in 1989 (<a name=\"InR38\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R38\">Irwin et al. 1989</a>; <a name=\"InR100\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R100\">Vanderriest et al. 1989</a>), and has become a powerful astrophysical tool in the current century (e.g. <a name=\"InR83\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>). Microlensing particularly affects compact sources such as the X-ray emitting regions, the accretion disk or the innermost line emitting clouds, so microlensed quasars are being intensively used to probe the quasar structure from spectral studies (e.g. <a name=\"InR12\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R12\">Chartas et al. 2004</a>; <a name=\"InR79\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R79\">Richards et al. 2004</a>; <a name=\"InR74\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R74\">Pooley et al. 2007</a>; <a name=\"InR87\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R87\">Sluse et al. 2007</a>; <a name=\"InR2\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R2\">Bate et al. 2008</a>; <a name=\"InR26\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R26\">Floyd et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR3\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R3\">Blackburne et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR51\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R51\">Mediavilla et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR60\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R60\">Mosquera et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR64\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R64\">Mu&#241;oz et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR39\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R39\">Jim&#233;nez-Vicente et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR62\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR89\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R89\">Sluse et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR34\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R34\">Guerras et al. 2013</a>; <a name=\"InR7\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R7\">Braibant et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR40\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R40\">Jim&#233;nez-Vicente et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR80\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R80\">Rojas et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR90\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R90\">Sluse et al. 2015</a>) and analyses of extrinsic variabilities (e.g. <a name=\"InR11\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R11\">Chartas et al. 2002</a>; <a name=\"InR86\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R86\">Shalyapin et al. 2002</a>; <a name=\"InR32\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R32\">Goicoechea et al. 2003</a>; <a name=\"InR43\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R43\">Kochanek 2004</a>; <a name=\"InR28\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R28\">Gil-Merino et al. 2006</a>; <a name=\"InR54\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R54\">Morgan et al. 2006</a>, <a name=\"InR55\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R55\">2008a</a>,<a name=\"InR56\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R56\">b</a>; <a name=\"InR68\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R68\">Paraficz et al. 2006</a>; <a name=\"InR21\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R21\">Eigenbrod et al. 2008</a>; <a name=\"InR73\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R73\">Poindexter et al. 2008</a>; <a name=\"InR13\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R13\">Chartas et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR18\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R18\">Dai et al. 2010</a>; <a name=\"InR57\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R57\">Morgan et al. 2010</a>; <a name=\"InR71\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R71\">Poindexter &amp; Kochanek 2010</a>; <a name=\"InR15\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R15\">Chen et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR88\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R88\">Sluse et al. 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR14\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R14\">Chartas et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR16\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R16\">Chen et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR58\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R58\">Morgan et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR35\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R35\">Hainline et al. 2013</a>; <a name=\"InR61\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R61\">Mosquera et al. 2013</a>; <a name=\"InR4\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R4\">Blackburne et al. 2014</a>, <a name=\"InR5\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R5\">2015</a>; <a name=\"InR48\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R48\">MacLeod et al. 2015</a>; <a name=\"InR52\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R52\">Mediavilla et al. 2015a</a>,<a name=\"InR53\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R53\">b</a>). </p>\n<p>Unfortunately, many lensed quasars have only experienced weak microlensing effects in their spectra and light curves. Thus, it is important to identify the systems showing substantial spectral distortions due to microlensing, sharp microlensing variations or both phenomena. These microlensing factories are excellent tools for detailed interpretations, as well as the best targets for subsequent follow-up through suitable facilities (e.g. <a name=\"InR59\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R59\">Mosquera &amp; Kochanek 2011</a>). For example, some systems exhibited strong chromatic microlensing in the optical continuum, with a maximum signal of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.8 mag at 5439 &#197; (HE 0047&#8211;1756 and SDSS J1155+6346; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R80\">Rojas et al. 2014</a>). A microlensing-induced distortion of the shape of several high-ionization emission lines was also unambiguously detected in SDSS J1004+4112 (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R79\">Richards et al. 2004</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>). Additionally, the Einstein Cross (QSO 2237+0305) displayed prominent microlensing (extrinsic) events in its <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>V</i></span>-band light curves over the final years of the past century (<a name=\"InR103\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R103\">Wo&#378;niak et al. 2000</a>; <a name=\"InR97\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R97\">Udalski et al. 2006</a>), and these <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.7 mag variations on hundreds of days strongly stimulated interpretation tasks and further observations (see above). </p>\n<p>Lensed quasars are not only powerful laboratories of the quasar structure, but are also often used as a cosmological probe (e.g. <a name=\"InR78\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R78\">Refsdal 1964</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>). For that purpose, the time delay between pairs of lensed images must be measured to high accuracy, requiring us to disentangle extrinsic and intrinsic variability. Intrinsic variations in a lensed quasar appear in two given images at different observing times, and a measurement of the time delay between these lensed images can be used to estimate the current expansion rate of the Universe (the so-called Hubble constant) and other cosmological parameters (e.g. <a name=\"InR67\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R67\">Oguri 2007</a>; <a name=\"InR92\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R92\">Suyu et al. 2010</a>, <a name=\"InR93\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R93\">2013</a>; <a name=\"InR84\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R84\">Sereno &amp; Paraficz 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR77\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R77\">Rathna Kumar et al. 2015</a>). The time delay is also sensitive to the distribution of the lensing mass (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R78\">Refsdal 1964</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>; <a name=\"InR29\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R29\">Goicoechea &amp; Shalyapin 2010</a>), so time delay measurements are turning in critical data for cosmology and extragalactic astrophysics. However, lensed quasars with appreciable extrinsic variations in their optical light curves may be a challenge for time delay determinations, since, in general, the extrinsic variability should be modelled by microlensing simulations or appropriate functions (e.g. <a name=\"InR94\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>, and references therein). </p>\n<p>Various techniques have been developed to deblend the intrinsic signal from the microlensing variability. However, as seems intuitive, if the intrinsic and extrinsic variations have similar timescales and amplitudes, it is not possible to fairly distinguish between both signals and accurately determine the time delay of a lens system (e.g. Q J0158&#8211;4325; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R55\">Morgan et al. 2008a</a>, <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R58\">2012</a>). Despite the presence of clear extrinsic variability in HE 1104&#8211;1805, the time delay between its two images was measured to 4% precision (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence interval; <a name=\"InR66\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R66\">Ofek &amp; Maoz 2003</a>). <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R66\">Ofek &amp; Maoz (2003)</a> modelled a long-term microlensing gradient and analised the influence of short-timescale microlensing events (having a mean amplitude of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.07 mag and a duration of approximately 1 month) in the delay estimation. Using additional data, and Legendre polynomials for describing the intrinsic and extrinsic variabilities, the relative uncertainty in the time delay measurement was decreased to 2% (<a name=\"InR72\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R72\">Poindexter et al. 2007</a>). For this system (HE 1104&#8211;1805), <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R55\">Morgan et al. (2008a)</a> also re-estimate the delay by modelling the extrinsic fluctuations through microlensing simulations. They obtained an 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence interval in very good agreement with the Ofek &amp; Maoz&#8217;s measurement. More recently, <a name=\"InR95\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R95\">Tewes et al. (2013b)</a> and <a name=\"InR76\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R76\">Rathna Kumar et al. (2013)</a> modelled the long-timescale extrinsic variations of two lens systems (RX J1131&#8211;1231 and SDSS J1001+5027) by free-knot splines (among other techniques), incorporating short-timescale correlated noise in their analyses. They measured the longest delays with <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8804;</span>2% precision. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R35\">Hainline et al. (2013)</a> also modelled the microlensing fluctuations in SBS 0909+532 by intensive simulations. The light curves of this double quasar included an intrinsic deep dip and significant extrinsic variability on different timescales, allowing the authors to estimate the 50-d delay with 6% precision. </p>\n<p>As a part of the road map to deeply analyse the system <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a>, this paper is mainly dedicated to characterising the microlensing signal in the wavelength and time domains, as well as to measure the time delay. The lens system <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> consists of two quasar images (A and B) at the same redshift <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>s</sub></span> = 2.231 and separated by 1.&#779;70, as well as a lensing galaxy (G) at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub></span> = 0.609 and located 0.&#779;63 from the B image (<a name=\"InR37\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R37\">Inada et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR85\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Shalyapin &amp; Goicoechea 2014</a>). While A and B are optically bright (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i> ~ 18&#8211;19</span> mag), the galaxy G has an <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band magnitude of about 20.5. Spectra of the lens system were obtained in 2013 using the OSIRIS R500R grism on the 10.4 m Gran Telescopio Canarias (GTC). These GTC-OSIRIS spectra allowed us to measure the redshift of G and obtain constraints on the macrolens and extinction parameters (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Shalyapin &amp; Goicoechea 2014</a>, henceforth Paper I). We also found evidence for strong chromatic microlensing in its optical continuum (reaching a maximum signal of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1.5&#8211;1.7 mag at <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5000 &#197;), and were able to predict a time delay between A and B of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>40&#8211;50 d (A is leading). The presence of sharp extrinsic events in preliminary light curves with the 2.0 m Liverpool Telescope (LT) precluded direct measurement of the delay. </p>\n<p>In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S2\">2</a>, we present new high-quality GTC (optical) spectra of A, B and G in 2014. In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S3\">3</a>, we discuss widely the several spectral distortions caused by microlensing. In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S7\">4</a>, we also present <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band LT light curves of A and B in 2009, 2012&#8211;2015 and early 2016. In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S8\">5</a>, these curves are used to estimate the time delay and the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band microlensing variability. Our conclusions are summarised in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S12\">6</a>. We also include two appendices to address some specific issues. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S2\"></a>2. Spatially resolved spectroscopy</h2>\n<p>To improve the spectroscopic information of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a>, 3570 (3<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>1190) s GTC-OSIRIS exposures of the lens system were taken on 27 March 2014 (R500R grism) and 20 May 2014 (R500B grism). The slit was oriented along the line joining both quasar images A and B, and the slit width was 1.&#779;23 (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5 pixel). We used IRAF<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN4\">1</a></sup> packages to perform usual data reductions, and the raw and reduced spectral frames in FITS format are publicly available at the GTC archive<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN5\">2</a></sup>. The new <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1 h exposures are longer than the previous one with the R500R grism (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>). The new data also cover a broader wavelength range of 3600&#8211;9260 &#197; (the fringing and second-order contamination become relevant from about 9300 &#197; redward; see Sects. 6.3 and 6.7 of <a name=\"InR9\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R9\">Cabrera-Lavers 2014</a>). Additionally, the seeing conditions in 2014 were better than those in 2013, since we estimated seeing values of 0.&#779;8, 0.&#779;9 and 1.&#779;1 for the three dithered sub-exposures with the red grism (7000 &#197;), and 0.&#779;9, 0.&#779;9 and 1.&#779;0 for the sub-exposures with the blue grism (4745 &#197;). The spectral resolution of the two grisms was evaluated from the widths of the bright sky line at 5557 &#197;, resulting in resolving powers of 290 (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>FWHM</i> = 19.2</span> &#197;; red grism R500R) and 367 (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>FWHM</i></span> = 15.2 &#197;; blue grism R500B). </p>\n<p>Before presenting the extraction of spectra for the three sources (A, B and G), we want to briefly discuss the differential atmospheric refraction (DAR) in our observations. This atmospheric phenomenon may cause a substantial chromatic offset of sources across the slit (transverse direction), leading to undesirable spectral artefacts (<a name=\"InR24\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R24\">Filippenko 1982</a>). The amplitude of the wavelength-dependent transverse offset is related to the airmass and the slit position angle with respect to the zenith. Thus, the higher is the airmass and the farther the zenith is oriented the slit, the larger is the amplitude of the transverse offset. For the three GTC-OSIRIS sub-exposures with the red grism, the values of the airmass and the slit position angle were (1.223, 1.294, 1.383) and (&#8211;1.&#778;8, &#8211;4.&#778;0, &#8211;5.&#778;6), respectively. For the blue grism sub-exposures, the airmass was lower (1.056, 1.076, 1.105), while the position angle was larger (24.&#778;9, 15.&#778;2, 8.&#778;2). From these data, considering on-axis quasar images at 6200 &#197; (acquisition frames were taken in the SDSS <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> passband), the maximum transverse shifts of sources are 0.&#779;05 (red grism) and 0.&#779;2 (blue grism), and significant spectral distortions induced by DAR are not expected. The maximum spectral deviation by slit loss occurs on the blue edge of the blue grism, where a transverse shift of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span> 0.&#779;2 induces a deviation of a few percent. This artefact is removed when calculating the spectral ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i>/<i>A</i></span> in the bluest region. The spectral ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i>/<i>A</i></span> is essentially free from DAR artefacts, because the two involved spectra are equally affected (same grism) or weakly affected (region of overlap between both grisms at 4850&#8211;7200 &#197;) by DAR. </p>\n<p>\n        <a name=\"F1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig1_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 1</span></a><p>GTC-OSIRIS spectra of SDSS J1339+1310ABG in 2014. The fluxes from the R500R (27 March) and R500B (20 May) grism data are depicted with bright and light colours, respectively (A in red, B in blue and G in green). Vertical dotted lines indicate emission lines at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>s</sub></span> = 2.231, while grey highlighted regions are associated with atmospheric artefacts.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=1&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n      </p>\n<p>The extraction of individual spectra of A, B and G is not a so direct task, and we followed the procedure that was presented and exhaustively discussed in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>. Our technique is a variant of the approach by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R87\">Sluse et al. (2007)</a> to produce spectra of sources in a crowded region. We initially modelled the lens system as a 2D light distribution including two point-like sources (A and B) and a de Vaucouleurs profile (G). This ideal model relied on the astro-photometric constraints in the last column of Table 1 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>. The initial light distribution was then convolved with a 2D Moffat function, masked with the slit transmission and integrated across the slit. The final (realistic) 1D model at each wavelength depends on the position of A, the shape of the Moffat profile, and the fluxes of A, B and G. After some iterations (fits to the 1D data for each wavelength bin), we extracted the instrumental fluxes of the three sources throughout the entire spectral range of each individual frame (sub-exposure). The basic response curves (red and blue grisms) to calibrate the flux scales were derived from spectroscopic observations of the standard stars Hilt600 and GD153. Each star was modelled as a 2D Moffat distribution at each wavelength. These distributions were masked with the slit transmission, integrated across the slit and compared to the 1D observed profiles. The basic responses were then corrected by airmass differences to get a response curve for each individual frame of the lens system. In a last step, the three instrumental spectra of each source for each grism were calibrated in flux and combined into a single spectral energy distribution. </p>\n<p>The final spectra of A, B and G are available in tabular format at the CDS<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN6\">3</a></sup>: Table 1 includes wavelengths in &#197; (Col. 1), and fluxes of A, B and G in 10<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-17</sup></span> erg&#8201;cm<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> &#197;<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> (Cols. 2&#8211;4) from the red grism data, while Table 2 is structured in the same manner as Table 1, but incorporating the spectra associated with the blue grism. The results from the observations with both grisms are also plotted in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a>. In this figure, we show the spectra of A (red), B (blue) and G (green). Bright colours trace the R500R spectra at the first observing epoch, whereas light colours represent the R500B spectra at the second epoch, <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>50 d later. As expected, the new quasar spectra are much less noisy than those in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, and incorporate many emission lines (vertical dotted lines). Here, in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S3\">3</a>, we focus on the five resolved prominent features: Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>], C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>. Although a detailed study of the quasar absorption lines is out of the scope of this paper, several absorption features are also evident in the spectra of A and B. In Appendix <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#APP1\">A</a>, we assess the quality of the spectral extraction, as well as the short-term evolution of the quasar spectral energy distribution. </p>\n<p>\n        \n        <a name=\"F2\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig2_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 2</span></a><p>GTC-OSIRIS spectra of SDSS J1339+1310G in 2014 and the early-type galaxy template from the SDSS database. The fluxes from the R500R and R500B grism data are shown in bright and light green, respectively. We note that the spectral edges are particularly noisy and not included here. The red-shifted (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i> = 0.607</span>) spectral template is drawn with magenta lines. Vertical dashed lines are associated with absorption features, while grey highlighted regions are related to atmospheric artefacts.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=2&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n      </p>\n<p>Regarding the lensing galaxy, we analysed the new spectra of G in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a> to better understand its nature. The RVSAO/XCSAO IRAF package (<a name=\"InR46\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R46\">Kurtz &amp; Mink 1998</a>) was used to perform the cross-correlation between these spectra and two different early-type galaxy templates. First, the improved spectrum from the red grism and the elliptical galaxy template in the Kinney-Calzetti Spectral Atlas of Galaxies (<a name=\"InR42\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R42\">Kinney et al. 1996</a>) led to a signal-to-noise ratio in the cross-correlation peak (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>cc</sub></span>; <a name=\"InR96\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R96\">Tonry &amp; Davis 1979</a>) of about 5, while <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>cc</sub></span> reached a value of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>8 using the spectrum from the blue grism. Second, taking the early-type galaxy template in the SDSS database<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN7\">4</a></sup>, we obtained <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>cc</sub></span> values in the interval 6&#8211;7. The four measurements of the lens redshift (one for each combination spectrum-template) ranged between 0.607 and 0.608. In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F2\">2</a>, we compare the two galaxy spectra with the red-shifted SDSS template (adopting <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub> = 0.607</span>; see below). These GTC-OSIRIS spectra clearly show several absorption features (e.g. the Ca&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>HK</i></span> doublet and the G-band are unambiguously detected with both grisms), which allowed us to estimate <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub></span> values close to 0.606. Thus, the data of G in 2014 lead to <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub> = 0.607 &#177; 0.001</span> (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> interval). Although the new typical value of the lens redshift differs only in a 0.3% from the previous one in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, we adopt the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub></span> for the improved spectra. We did not try to fit stellar population synthesis models to the spectra, but the lack of [O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>] emission at 3727 &#197; (it would be observed at <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>6000 &#197;) is likely indicating a lack of star formation in the lensing galaxy (e.g. <a name=\"InR41\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R41\">Kennicutt 1998</a>). </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S3\"></a>3. Microlensing in the broad line and nuclear continuum emitting regions</h2>\n<p>GTC-OSIRIS-R500R spectroscopy of the doubly imaged quasar <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> in 2013, revealed the presence of strong chromatic microlensing of its continuum, which is primarily generated in a nuclear region. In <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, we used the flux ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i>/<i>A</i></span> for the cores of three emission lines (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>) to estimate the macrolens magnification and dust extinction ratios, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i><sub>BA</sub></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>BA</sub></span>, and thus find the microlensing magnification ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub></span> of light basically coming from the nuclear continuum emitting region (NCER). Our main hypothesis in this first paper was that the cores of emission lines are dominated by photons arising from the narrow line emitting region (NLER) and the outer parts of the broad line emitting region (BLER). Therefore, the line cores would be produced in extended regions that are unaffected by microlensing (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>, and references therein). </p>\n<p>The GTC-OSIRIS low-noise spectra of the lensed quasar in 2014 cover the whole range of optical wavelengths and contain five prominent emission features, that is the three previous lines and two additional features at bluer wavelengths (Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> and Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>]; see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a>). These new data offer a much richer information than those in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, and are useful to check the hypothesis about microlensing-free fluxes of line cores (see Sects. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S4\">3.1</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S5\">3.2</a>), as well as to improve our knowledge on the extinction and magnification in <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> (see Sects. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S5\">3.2</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S6\">3.3</a>). We considered a number of details to analyse the optical spectra of A and B, including an accurate estimation of the total and nuclear power-law continua. The spectral energy distributions of the A image at 4850&#8211;7200 &#197; for the two spectroscopy epochs in 2014 are remarkably similar (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a> and Appendix <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#APP1\">A</a>), so we mainly used the blue grism data to estimate delay-corrected flux ratios over a very broad wavelength range (3600&#8211;7200 &#197;; it was implicitly assumed that the observed constancy of the spectrum of A in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>g</i></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> bands can be extended to the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>u</i></span> band). These delay-corrected ratios were complemented with two measurements from the red grism data: flux ratios of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line cores (a justification for the use of the two additional single-epoch ratios is provided at the end of Appendix <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#APP1\">A</a>). </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S4\"></a>3.1. Microlensing-free fluxes of line cores?</h3>\n<p>We initially assumed that the line cores are unaffected by microlensing and only macrolens-extinction effects are playing a role (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>, and references therein). In this scenario, due to differential dust extinction in the lensing galaxy, the line-core flux ratios and the associated magnitude differences are expected to have a certain chromatic behaviour. Using a standard formalism for the differential extinction of lensed images (e.g. <a name=\"InR23\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R23\">Falco et al. 1999</a>; <a name=\"InR104\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R104\">Wucknitz et al. 2003</a>; <a name=\"InR22\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R22\">El&#237;asd&#243;ttir et al. 2006</a>), we presented two chromaticity laws in Eqs. (2) and (3) of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, which can be compared with the observed magnitude differences in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>. Although we were able to perfectly fit the Galactic law to the new data for the cores of the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>, C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span> emissions (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup> ~ 0</span>; see the dotted red line in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">3</a>), a large value of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> was obtained by adding the magnitude difference for Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup> ~</span>  10 with 1 degree of freedom; see the dashed red line in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">3</a>). The fitting result was even worse when we used the five available differences (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup> ~</span> 80 with 2 degrees of freedom; see the solid red line in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">3</a>). </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F3\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig3_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 3</span></a><p>Line-core magnitude differences for SDSS J1339+1310. Filled circles with error bars describe our measurements in the fifth column of Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>. Instead of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span> values, the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i></span>-axis displays the wavelength-dependent quantity <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i> &#8733; 1 /<i>&#955;</i></span> (see main text). Assuming microlensing-free fluxes of line cores and Galactic extinction in the lensing galaxy, we also show the best fits using Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>+ C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>data (dotted red line), Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>+ C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>+ Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] data (dashed red line) and the five measurements (solid red line). For a standard linear extinction in the lensing galaxy, the solid green line traces the best fit to the five magnitude differences.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=3&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>Apart from the Galactic extinction (<a name=\"InR10\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R10\">Cardelli et al. 1989</a>), we also considered the simple linear law: <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>a</i> + <i>bx</i></span>, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i> = (1 + <i>z</i><sub>l</sub>) /<i>&#955;</i></span>, as a second variant. This is a reasonable approach for dust extinction at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i> ~ 2</span>&#8211;4 (e.g. <a name=\"InR75\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R75\">Pr&#233;vot et al. 1984</a>), that is in the spectral region of interest (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">3</a>). The standard linear law did not fit significantly better than the Galactic law, since the five magnitude differences in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a> produced a still unacceptable chi-square value (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>80 with 3 degrees of freedom; see the solid green line in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">3</a>). Therefore, our analysis indicated the existence of a conflict between a macrolens-extinction scenario and the full dataset of <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>core</sub></span> values, and thus revealed that microlensing is affecting to some extent the emission line cores. Although we assumed that the line central features are dominated by photons arising from the NLER and the outer parts of the BLER, some of them contain an appreciable number of photons coming from clouds at inner zones of the BLER and moving almost perpendicularly to the line of sight (small projected motions). </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F4\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a><p>Multi-component decomposition of carbon line profiles and microlensing of emission lines. The <i>top and middle panels </i>display decompositions into four (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] narrow + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] broad + Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + Al&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>) and three (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>narrow + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>broad + He&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>complex) Gaussians, respectively. The <i>bottom panel </i>incorporates the microlensing magnification ratios of the line cores: decomposition of carbon lines (filled black circles; see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S5\">3.2</a>), standard stratification of the BLER (green circles; see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S4\">3.1</a>) and non-standard stratification of the BLER (red circles; see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S4\">3.1</a>). This last panel also shows the microlensing of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>broad emission lines (open black circles).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=4&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>The BLER is likely stratified, so different atoms or ions are located at different distances from the NCER. For example, there is evidence for a correlation between degree of ionization and distance, with the high-ionization species much closer to the NCER than those of low-ionization (e.g. <a name=\"InR44\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R44\">Krolik et al. 1991</a>; <a name=\"InR27\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R27\">Gaskell et al. 2007</a>). In this standard stratification of the BLER (SSB), the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> cores could suffer slight microlensing effects, while the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] cores would probably be free of microlensing (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). Hence, the low-ionization, outer lines of Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] were used to obtain a macrolens-extinction solution (standard linear law), and remove macrolens and extinction effects in the cores of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>  emissions. The best-fit solution was <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i><sub>BA</sub> = 0.16</span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>BA</sub>(<i>V</i>) = 1.44</span>, and the microlensing magnification ratios of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> line cores are shown in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a> (filled green circles). As expected, all the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core)</span> values are close to one. </p>\n<p>In addition to a SSB, we also considered a non-standard stratification of the BLER (NSB): the typical distance from the central engine to a specie depends on the total energy that is required to produce each excited or ionised atom of such specie (the higher the energy of excitation or ionization, the distance is smaller). This naive scenario relies on the idea that the available amount of energy for excitation or ionization decreases with distance, leading to outer lines of Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>  (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). We used these two lines to infer a new macrolens-extinction solution, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i><sub>BA</sub> = 0.19</span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>BA</sub>(<i>V</i>) = 1.22</span>, which allowed us to discuss the microlensing magnification ratios of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>], C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>and Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] line cores. It is noteworthy that we assumed <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core) = 1</span> (absence of microlensing) for the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> lines (excitation energy of hydrogen and ionization energy of magnesium of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>10 eV; see the open red circles in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>), and then obtained a microlensing  signal <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core) &gt; 1</span> for the other lines (see the filled red circles in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>), with a clear correlation between the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i> = <i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core)&#8722;1</span>  values and the ionization energies (i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i>/<i>E</i><sub>ion</sub> ~ 0.08/35</span> (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]), 0.34/80 (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>) and 0.25/60 (Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>]) eV<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span>). This correlation is fully consistent with the NSB scenario. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S5\"></a>3.2. Microlensing in the BLER</h3>\n<p>To estimate a reliable macrolens-extinction solution, the ideal procedure is to use pure narrow lines arising from the NLER (e.g. <a name=\"InR63\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R63\">Moustakas &amp; Metcalf 2003</a>), instead of line cores presumably unaffected by microlensing (e.g. the SSB and NSB scenarios give two solutions slightly different from each other; see above). Unfortunately, these pure narrow lines are not available yet, so we performed the decomposition of the carbon lines to get a robust solution. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission lines are located within the central region of the full range of wavelengths, and their signal strengths are enough as to do decompositions into broad and narrow components. With a little more detail, our decomposition is based on the blue grism spectra of the lensed quasar <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a>. After de-redshifting the spectra to their rest frame, apart from continuum subtractions, each line profile was modelled as a sum of Gaussian contributions (see below). A multi-component decomposition of line profiles has been used in many previous works with different aims (e.g. <a name=\"InR102\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R102\">Wills et al. 1985</a>; <a name=\"InR45\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R45\">Kuraszkiewicz et al. 2002</a>; <a name=\"InR20\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R20\">Dietrich et al. 2003</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R87\">Sluse et al. 2007</a>; <a name=\"InR49\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R49\">Marziani et al. 2010</a>), and recently, <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R88\">Sluse et al. (2011)</a> identified and studied different components of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>lines in spectra of the gravitationally lensed quasar Q2237+0305. </p>\n<p>To reproduce the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span>1909 profile in each quasar image, after some probes, we used two C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] components (broad and narrow), plus two blue-wing excesses due to Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span>1892 and Al&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii </span><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span>1857 emissions (e.g. <a name=\"InR8\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R8\">Brotherton et al. 1994</a>). Each of these four Gaussian components is characterised by three parameters: central wavelength <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub></span>, width (standard deviation) and amplitude. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>], Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Al&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii </span>emissions were fixed at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub> = 1908.7</span>, 1892.0 and 1857.4 &#197;, respectively. We then fitted the model to the A image data. To fit the B image data, the width of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] narrow component was set to be equal to that of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] narrow component in A (i.e. 4.8 &#197;), while the rest of parameters (seven) were allowed to vary. Our 4-Gaussian fits: C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] narrow + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] broad + Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + Al&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>, are shown in the top panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>. These fits yield <span class=\"simple-math\">(B/A)<sub>narrow</sub> = 0.239</span> and <span class=\"simple-math\">(B/A)<sub>broad</sub> = 0.384</span>. </p>\n<p>Regarding the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span>1549 line profiles, we used 3-Gaussian fits: C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>narrow + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>broad + He&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>complex. The so-called He&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>complex is a blend of several lines that is described as a single Gaussian centred at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub> = 1640.7</span> &#197; (e.g. <a name=\"InR17\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R17\">Croom et al. 2002</a>). Although we probed more sophisticated descriptions of this complex (e.g. <a name=\"InR25\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R25\">Fine et al. 2010</a>), the best-fit parameters for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission did not change in a significant way. Thus, we think the single-Gaussian model is enough to account for contamination. The fits with three components and eight free parameters are depicted in the middle panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>, and resulted in the flux ratios <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>narrow</sub> = 0.260</span> and <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>broad</sub> = 0.523</span>. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>narrow components in the two images have very similar position-structure parameters, since their central wavelengths only deviate from each other by <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1 &#197; (i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub>(A) = 1548.9</span> &#197; and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub>(B) = 1550.0</span> &#197;), and their widths differ in <span class=\"simple-math\">&lt;</span>1 &#197; (6.5 &#197; for A and 7.3 &#197; for B). Therefore, the narrow component in B does not seem to be noticeably affected by the line profile deformation that we discuss here below. We also note that there is a significant difference of 8.5 &#197; between the central wavelengths of the two broad components: <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub>(A) = 1544.0</span> &#197; and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i><sub>c</sub>(B) = 1552.5</span> &#197;. </p>\n<p>Our multi-component decomposition gives two narrow-line flux ratios that can be used to determine a reliable macrolens-extinction solution. This solution was <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i><sub>BA</sub> = 0.17</span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>BA</sub>(<i>V</i>) = 1.28</span>, amid those from the SSB and NSB approaches in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S4\">3.1</a>. As <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>BA</sub>(<i>V</i>)</span> is the dust extinction ratio in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>V</i></span>  band (i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#1013;</i><sub>B</sub>(<i>V</i>) /<i>&#1013;</i><sub>A</sub>(<i>V</i>)</span>), we find that A is the most reddened image. After removing macrolens and extinction effects in the broad-line and line-core flux ratios, in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>, we show new microlensing signals <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(broad)</span> (open black circles) and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core)</span> (filled black circles). The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>and Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] line cores are clearly affected by microlensing, and there is reasonable agreement between the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core)</span> values from the decomposition of carbon line profiles and the NSB. From the SSB scenario, we inferred biased microlensing ratios of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> line cores (filled green circles in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>). This bias is due to the wrong assumption <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(core) = 1</span> for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line (open green circle), which is not supported by the decomposition in the top and middle panels of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F4\">4</a>. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] BLER is not so extended as assumed in our framework for the SSB, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(broad) = 1.6</span> for this line. We also detected an important microlensing effect on the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>BLER, since B is amplified (relative to A) by a factor of two. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F5\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig5_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 5</span></a><p>C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span> emission line profiles for SDSS J1339+1310. The red line represents the profile for A, and the blue line traces the profile for B multiplied by a factor to match the line core of A. A power-law continuum has been subtracted from the spectra of A and B (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R79\">Richards et al. 2004</a>).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=5&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>In general, the BLER of lensed quasars suffers weak microlensing effects (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R34\">Guerras et al. 2013</a>). However, the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>BLER of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> is notably microlensed, which may produce a C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line profile for B different to that for A (e.g. <a name=\"InR65\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R65\">Nemiroff 1988</a>; <a name=\"InR81\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R81\">Schneider &amp; Wambsganss 1990</a>; <a name=\"InR1\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R1\">Abajas et al. 2002</a>; <a name=\"InR47\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R47\">Lewis &amp; Ibata 2004</a>). This microlensing-induced distortion of the line shape was observed in the quadruple quasar SDSS J1004+4112 at several epochs: when comparing the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission line in its images A and B, the A image shows blue wing enhancements and red wing diminutions (relative to B; e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R79\">Richards et al. 2004</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>). Here, we also checked whether this type of distortion occurs or not in <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a>. In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F5\">5</a>, the red line traces the profile for A, and the blue line describes the profile for B multiplied by the inverse of the line-core flux ratio in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>. Despite good agreement between both blue wings, there is a strong enhancement in the red wing of B relative to that of A. This result indicates that the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>BLER of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> does not have a spherically symmetric structure (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R89\">Sluse et al. 2012</a>, and references therein). </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F6\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F6.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig6_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F6.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 6</span></a><p>Microlensing magnification ratio of the nuclear continuum. We use the best power-law fits to the continuum of A and B (black line), as well as the 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> bands for these fits (grey region).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=6&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S6\"></a>3.3. Microlensing in the NCER</h3>\n<p>In comparison with the BLER, the NCER should be more strongly affected by microlenses because its smaller size (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R81\">Schneider &amp; Wambsganss 1990</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>). In <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, we discussed the microlensing magnification ratio of the continuum at <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5000&#8211;9000 &#197;, and found that B is amplified (relative to A) by a factor of about 3&#8211;5, with larger amplifications at shorter wavelengths. However, in this first analysis, we considered the total continuum from the NCER and the BLER (Balmer + iron forest), and the time delay between images was not taken into account. Our new spectra at two epochs separated by <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>50 d (see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S2\">2</a>), allowed us to properly compare the continuum of A and B. In the overlapping spectral region between the blue and red grisms, the spectra for A were identical at both epochs. Hence, we have concentrated on the blue grism spectra for A and B to estimate the ratio <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>cont</sub></span>. The trailing image B at a given observing time <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>t</i></span> is thus compared to the leading image A at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>t</i>&#8722;&#916;<i>t</i></span>, where <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i> ~ 50</span> d basically coincides with the predicted and measured time delay (see Sects. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S1\">1</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S8\">5</a>). To calculate <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>cont</sub></span>, we used the nuclear power-law continuum instead of the total one. Moreover, the macrolens-extinction effects were corrected through the solution in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S5\">3.2</a>. The final microlensing spectrum <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(cont)</span> is shown in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F6\">6</a>. In this figure, we see the expected chromaticity, with a typical magnification ratio of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5.7 at 5000 &#197; (i.e. about three times <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(broad)</span> for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission line). The microlensing signal <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub>(cont)</span> in <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> clearly exceeds those observed in most of lensed quasars for which <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub>(cont) &lt; 4</span> (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>i</i></span> = A, B... and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>j</i></span> = A, B... with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>i</i> &#8800; <i>j</i></span>; e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R89\">Sluse et al. 2012</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R80\">Rojas et al. 2014</a>). </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F7\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F7.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig7_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F7.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 7</span></a><p><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band LT imaging of SDSS J1339+1310. After combining the best RATCam frames in 2009 (total exposure time =6900 s), we added labels to the two quasar images (A and B) and the most relevant stars in the field of view: PSF star (PSF) and control star (star).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=7&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S7\"></a>4. Light curves in the <i>r</i> band</h2>\n<p>Immediately after the discovery of the double quasar <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R37\">Inada et al. 2009</a>), we began a robotic monitoring programme with the LT in the SDSS <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> passband (<a name=\"InR30\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R30\">Goicoechea &amp; Shalyapin 2012</a>). Our photometric observations were performed in 2009 and 2012&#8211;2015, and from 13 January to 8 February 2016, so they span five seasons of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>4&#8211;6 months each (tipically from January or February to July) and the beginning of the current season. For most of the observing nights, we set the exposure time to 600 s. Usually this 600 s exposure was divided into two 300 s sub-exposures or four 150 s sub-exposures. The <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band frames in 2009 and 2012 were obtained with the RATCam optical CCD camera, which was decommissioned a few years ago. This camera had a <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq75.png\"></span> field of view and a pixel scale of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span><span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq76.png\"></span> (binning <span class=\"simple-math\">2 &#215; 2</span>). All observations in 2013&#8211;2016 were made using the new IO:O camera. IO:O is characterised by a <span class=\"simple-math\">10&#8242; &#215; 10&#8242;</span> field of view and a pixel scale of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span><span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq79.png\"></span> (binning <span class=\"simple-math\">2 &#215; 2</span>). Apart from basic pre-processing tasks included in the LT pipelines, we cleaned cosmic rays and interpolated over bad pixels using bad pixel masks. The global database consists of 392 individual frames<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN8\">5</a></sup>. </p>\n<p>At a first stage, a crowded-field photometry pipeline produced relative magnitudes of the quasar images in each individual frame. This photometric pipeline is based on IRAF tasks and the IMFITFITS software (<a name=\"InR50\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R50\">McLeod et al. 1998</a>). We obtained relative fluxes (magnitudes) of A and B through point-spread function (PSF) fitting, using the unsaturated star with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i> = 16.866</span> mag in the vicinity of the lens system as the PSF star<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN9\">6</a></sup> (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F7\">7</a>). This PSF star is also taken as reference for differential photometry. Our photometric model included A and B (two PSFs), the lensing galaxy G (a de Vaucouleurs profile convolved with the PSF) and a constant background. We also incorporated constraints in the last column of Table 1 of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>: the relative positions of B and G (with respect to A), and the structure parameters of G. In a first iteration, we applied the code to the frames with the best values of seeing and signal-to-noise ratio (S/N), allowing the galaxy-to-PSF star ratio (<i>G/PSF</i>) to be free. We then derived <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>G</i>/<i>PSF</i> = 0.035</span> (i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i> = 20.5</span> mag for G), and adopted this ratio in a second iteration. In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F7\">7</a>, we show a stacked frame of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> with the LT in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band. This includes, among other objects, the lens system (central region), as well as a control star with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> = 17.360 mag (down and to the left of the lens system) and the PSF star. </p>\n<p>In a second stage, we derived final light curves (SDSS magnitudes) for A, B and the control star. We have removed 26 individual frames leading to outliers in the initial light curves of the quasar. These outlier frames were discarded on basis of objective criteria. First, by visual inspection of the brightness records, we found that the magnitudes of A and B strongly deviate from adjacent data. Deviations of A and B occur in the same direction or in opposite directions (likely due to crosstalk between both images), and exceed by more than three times the preliminary typical errors. Second, a careful data analysis indicated that either the PSF fitting of the lens system led to a large <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> value, the image quality was poor or both things hapenned. After this selection of frames, we have combined magnitudes measured on the same night.
