In the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band, G is <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>2 mag fainter than B and the sky background does not play a dramatic role. Thus, even using a crude photometric model including only the two quasar images (two PSFs), we found light curves similar to those in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F8\">8</a>. </p>\n<p>\n        \n        <a name=\"F8\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F8.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig8_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F8.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 8</span></a><p>LT light curves of both quasar images and the control star. The stellar data are offset by +2.0 mag to facilitate comparison. The vertical dotted lines correspond to our spectroscopic observations with the GTC in 2013 and 2014.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=8&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n      </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S8\"></a>5. Time delay and <i>r</i>-band microlensing variability</h2>\n<p>As noted in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>, our original aim was to determine the time delay of the system from a few observing seasons, since the expected delay is less than two months. However, the LT light curves over the first seasons did not permit us to measure the delay between A and B. Now, in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F8\">8</a>, we see that A and B appreciably vary in parallel on a long timescale. This means that the long-timescale variations are primarily originated in the distant quasar (intrinsic origin). In addition, there is also clear evidence of microlensing (extrinsic) variability, for example several sharp events in B are not seen in A. Although a time delay  measurement in presence of microlensing is a relatively complex task, the extrinsic variability can be reasonably described either by a suitable type of functions (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>) or through intensive microlensing simulations (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R35\">Hainline et al. 2013</a>). The last method (simulations) was our initial option (see conclusions of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>), but it is computationally expensive, and provides results that are not better than those from the first technique (see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S1\">1</a>). Therefore, here, we focus on the first approach. This yields a microlensing light curve in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band, which, together with the microlensing spectrum in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F6\">6</a>, can be compared with numerical simulations. Such a comparison is out of the scope of this paper, despite its great interest to constrain the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band size and wavelength-dependent structure of the quasar accretion disk (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R2\">Bate et al. 2008</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R21\">Eigenbrod et al. 2008</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R57\">Morgan et al. 2010</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R60\">Mosquera et al. 2011</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R62\">Motta et al. 2012</a>). </p>\n<p>We use two different approaches to determine the time delay of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a>, one including seasonal microlensing variability (Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S9\">5.1</a>) and the other incorporating microlensing variations on all timescales (Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S10\">5.2</a>). These approaches and their associated methods are useful to track for systematic errors. The final measurement of the delay and the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band microlensing variability are discussed in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S11\">5.3</a>. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S9\"></a>5.1. Time delay: seasonal microlensing</h3>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"F9\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F9.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig9_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F9.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 9</span></a><p><span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span>-delay relationships for bin semisizes <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 15</span> (red), 20 (black), and 25 (green) d.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=9&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F10\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F10.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig10_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F10.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 10</span></a><p>Combined light curve in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band from the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> minimization with a step function-like microlensing. The A curve (red circles) and the magnitude- and time-shifted B curve (blue circles) are drawn together (see main text).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=10&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>We initially performed a reduced chi-square (<span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span>) minimization to match the light curves of A and B over the period 2009&#8211;2015. The data in early 2016 are only used to demonstrate the parallel brightening of both images in recent years. Our <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> method was based on a comparison between the curve A and the time-shifted curve B (shifted forward or backward in <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8804;</span>100 d), using bins with semisize <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> in B (e.g. <a name=\"InR98\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R98\">Ull&#225;n et al. 2006</a>). To account for the presence of long-timescale extrinsic variability, we incorporated six free parameters: a time delay (<span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub> = <i>&#964;</i><sub>B</sub>&#8722;<i>&#964;</i><sub>A</sub></span>) and a magnitude offset for each of the five seasons in 2009 and 2012&#8211;2015. From the formal point of view, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(<i>t</i>) = <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>(<i>t</i> + &#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub>)&#8722;<i>m</i><sub>A</sub>(<i>t</i>)</span> is assumed to be constant within a given season, but it can vary from season to season. After analysing the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span>-delay relationships for different values of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>, we found that <span class=\"simple-math\">10 &lt;<i>&#945;</i> &#8804; 30</span> d lead to reasonably smooth trends with global minima at 46&#8211;49 d (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F9\">9</a>). Thus, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 20</span> d gives a best solution <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub> = 47</span> d, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(2009) = &#8722;0.349</span> mag, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(2012) = &#8722;0.419</span> mag, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(2013) = &#8722;0.133</span> mag, <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(2014) = &#8722;0.118</span> mag and <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>AB</sub>(2015) = &#8722;0.304</span> mag (<span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq100.png\"></span>; see the black line in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F9\">9</a>). The combined light curve for this best solution is displayed in the five panels of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F10\">10</a>. The existence of short-timescale microlensing events in all seasons does not allow us to get a best fit with <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq101.png\"></span>, since the intra-seasonal microlensing works as an additional noise that is not taken into account in the denominator of the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> addends. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F11\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F11.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig11_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F11.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 11</span></a><p>Histograms from 1000 pairs of synthetic curves AB and the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> minimization (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 18&#8211;22</span> d) with a step function-like microlensing. The <i>top panel </i>shows the best solutions of the time delay. The <i>bottom panel </i>displays the best solutions of the magnitude offsets: 2009 (black), 2012 (red), 2013 (green), 2014 (blue) and 2015 (cyan).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=11&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>We used a simple approach to generate synthetic light curves and estimate parameter errors. To obtain a pair of synthetic curves AB, the observed magnitudes were modified by additive random quantities (i.e. realisations of normal distributions around zero, with standard deviations equal to the measured uncertainties). We produced 1000 pairs of synthetic curves, and the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span>  minimization (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 18&#8211;22</span> d) with a step function-like microlensing was applied to them. The distributions of delays and magnitude offsets are presented in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F11\">11</a>, and we pay special attention to the top panel including the delay histogram. Our 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> measurement of the delay is <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq103.png\"></span> d, while <span class=\"simple-math\">45 &#8804; &#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub> &#8804; 51</span> d is the 2<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> (95%) confidence interval. Although we only consider a 5-d interval of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>  values to account for the intrinsic variance of the technique, this is enough. From the LT light curves, we derived distributions of delays for <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 18&#8211;22</span> d and for a broader range of values of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> (12&#8211;28 d), and found similar standard deviations of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1 d. </p>\n<p>We also used the dispersion (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>D</i><sup>2</sup></span>) technique (<a name=\"InR69\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R69\">Pelt et al. 1994</a>, <a name=\"InR70\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R70\">1996</a>) to check for possible biases in the delay estimation from the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> method. The <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>D</i><sup>2</sup></span> minimization is characterised by a decorrelation length (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i></span>) and has several variants (mainly the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq107.png\"></span> and <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq108.png\"></span> estimators introduced by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R70\">Pelt et al. 1996</a>). The key idea of our <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> method is to take the light curve of A as a template for smooth (mainly intrinsic) variability, and compare the A magnitudes with binned magnitudes of B. However, the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>D</i><sup>2</sup></span> technique does not differenciate between A and B, and it works as a kind of average between the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq88.png\"></span> version that we used, and another complementary version based on a comparison between the B magnitudes and binned magnitudes of A. From the <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq107.png\"></span> estimator including a step function-like microlensing, we derived minima around an average value of 47.5 d (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#948;</i> = 25&#8211;35</span> d). These independent results confirm a time delay of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>47 d when only long-timescale extrinsic variability is taken into account. The influence of the microlensing model on the delay estimation is discussed in the next subsections. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S10\"></a>5.2. Time delay: spline-like microlensing</h3>\n<p>Although long-timescale microlensing is clearly detected and measured in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F11\">11</a>, there are also short-timescale microlensing events that were not modelled and might play a role in the determination of the time delay. In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F10\">10</a>, we see that extrinsic magnitude fluctuations on timescales of 50&#8211;100 d are usual, and they are even faster than the intra-seasonal intrinsisc variations. To account for this fast extrinsic variability, we used the PyCS software<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN10\">7</a></sup> (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>; <a name=\"InR6\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R6\">Bonvin et al. 2016</a>). Thus, the intrinsic and extrinsic variations were described by free-knot splines. Each cubic spline was parametrised by the knot epochs and the associated coefficients, and a <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> minimization allowed us to simultaneously fit the time delay and the splines. After some tests, we took a knot step <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>int</sub> = 100</span> d for the intrinsic spline because this step leads to a good fit of the light curve A. For the extrinsic spline, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>ext</sub> = 100</span> d gave rise to rough fits to the data: <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup> ~ 600</span> with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>N</i> = 2 &#215; 137 = 274</span> data points. The time delay through this rough spline-like microlensing was <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>47 d, in very good agreement with our initial measurement using a step function-like microlensing. </p>\n<p>We obtained good fits for a knot step <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>ext</sub> = 50</span> d, and a typical solution is shown in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F12\">12</a>. The black and blue lines represent the intrinsic and extrinsic splines, respectively (see caption). In the top and bottom panels of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F13\">13</a>, we also display <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> and delay values for 200 solutions with different initial conditions (time shifts of the observed curves). The <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> histogram is mainly concentrated within the 250&#8211;270 interval, and we only considered the solutions with <span class=\"simple-math\">250 &#8804; <i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup> &#8804; 270</span>. These are associated with plausible delays ranging from 41 to 52 d. Using free-knot splines, we are dealing with a complex non-linear optimization involving a large set of free parameters (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>), and this optimization sometimes yields biased results (local minima, degeneracies...). For example, our solutions with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup>&gt; 270</span> generate the delay wings below 40 d and above 53 d in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F13\">13</a>, and thus, we removed these presumably biased estimates. The distribution of plausible delays has a standard deviation of 1.8 d, and it defines the intrinsic variance of the method. While we obtain an intrinsic uncertainty below 2 d, the total uncertainty (initial conditions + photometric realisations) must be larger. Unfortunately, we were not able to fairly assess the total error from synthetic curves as those in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S9\">5.1</a>. Such synthetic curves led to a broad delay histogram and an ambiguous distribution of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span>  values, which did not permit us to reject biased solutions and select the unbiased ones. The PyCS software was not originally designed to work with extrinsic variations more rapid than intrinsic ones (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>), and its results seem to be less robust in that case. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F12\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F12.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig12_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F12.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 12</span></a><p>Combined light curve in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band from the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> minimization with spline-like microlensing. The A curve (red circles), the magnitude- and time-shifted B curve (blue circles), and the intrinsic spline (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>int</sub> = 100</span> d; black line) are drawn together. The extrinsic spline (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>ext</sub> = 50</span> d; blue line) describes the extrinsic variability correction that we apply to the curve B. The vertical ticks represent the knots.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=12&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S11\"></a>5.3. Adopted delay and microlensing light curve in the <i>r </i>band</h3>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"F13\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F13.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig13_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F13.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 13</span></a><p>Distributions from the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> minimization with spline-like microlensing. We fit the observed curves AB to splines with <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>int</sub> = 100</span> d and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#951;</i><sub>ext</sub> = 50</span> d, starting from randomised initial time shifts. The <i>top panel </i>shows <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> values for 200 solutions. The <i>bottom panel </i>shows the delay histogram for these solutions (see main text).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=13&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F14\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F14.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig14_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F14.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 14</span></a><p>Microlensing light curve in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band. Time evolution of the microlensing magnification ratio using three time delays: 41 d (red circles), 47 d (black circles) and 52 d (green circles), as well as 20-d bins (see main text).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=14&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>In the two previous sections, we have evaluated the time delay of <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> from its noisy light curves with extrinsic variability. A simple microlensing model exclusively including seasonal variability (short-timescale microlensing events are considered to be irrelevant in the delay estimation and are consequently ignored) produces an 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence interval of 46&#8211;49 d. This model is reasonable because the ignored events are faster than the intrinsic variations. Moreover, it has a relatively small number of free parameters. From the observed brightness records, a second approach consisting of an extrinsic free-knot spline leads to a plausible delay range of 41&#8211;52 d. Whereas this second model incorporates microlensing variations on all timescales, the number of free parameters is large, and degeneracies and other problems sometimes bring biased delays that must be removed from delay distributions. As there is no a fair procedure to remove biased solutions from synthetic light curves, we cannot obtain confidence intervals in a standard way for the realistic (but complex) model. Instead we use the plausible delay range from the observed light curves as a reasonable proxy to the 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> error bar. This can be justified from simulated light curves and a modest prior on their associated delay distribution. We concentrated on analysing the simulated delay histogram within the range from 30 to 60 d (assuming the prior that shorter and longer delays are unphysical solutions), and derived a mean value and a standard deviation in good agreement with the plausible delay range from the observations. As the new delay interval includes the first (based on a simple model incorporating seasonal microlensing variability), we adopt <span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq117.png\"></span> d as our final 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> measurement. </p>\n<p>Once the time delay is measured, we can construct the microlensing light curve in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band. First, we made the difference light curve: <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>m</i><sub>A</sub>(<i>t</i>)&#8722;<i>m</i><sub>B</sub>(<i>t</i> + &#916;<i>t</i><sub>AB</sub>)</span>, using 20-d bins and three delay values (41, 47 and 52 d). We then removed the macrolens and extinction contributions at 6225 &#197; (through the solution at the beginning of the four paragraph in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S5\">3.2</a>), and converted magnitude differences into flux ratios. As we see in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F14\">14</a>, the microlensing curve is not very sensitive to changes in the delay within the range 41&#8211;52 d. Moreover, this curve does not critically depend on the bin size. We checked shorter and longer bins, and did not find any discrepancy between the corresponding curves and the trends depicted in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F14\">14</a>. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S12\"></a>6. Conclusions</h2>\n<p>We presented GTC spectrophotometric data of the gravitational lens system <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> in 2014. As regards previous GTC-OSIRIS observations with the R500R grism at a single epoch (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>), the exposures in 2014 were taken with the R500R and R500B grisms at two epochs separated by approximately the time delay of the system. The seeing and the S/N were also better. The new spectra of the lensing galaxy G incorporate several absorption features (e.g. Ca&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>HK</i></span> doublet and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>G</i></span>-band) that helped us improve our previous lens redshift determination. We found a practically irrelevant offset of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub></span> by a &#8211;0.3%, so <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i><sub>l</sub> = 0.607 &#177; 0.001</span> (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span>) from the 2014 data. The new spectra of the quasar images A and B include five prominent emission lines (Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>], C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>), two of which were not formerly observed with the GTC. From a multi-component decomposition of the carbon line profiles (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R102\">Wills et al. 1985</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R45\">Kuraszkiewicz et al. 2002</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R49\">Marziani et al. 2010</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R88\">Sluse et al. 2011</a>), we derived two narrow-line flux  ratios B/A, which were then used to achieve a reliable macrolens-extinction solution for a standard (linear) extinction law in G. This solution allowed us to remove macrolens and extinction contributions in our quasar spectra. We note that the narrow components of the carbon lines are related to line emitting gas with typical velocities of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>700 (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]) and 1300 (C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>) km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span>, which belong to the inner NLER (e.g. <a name=\"InR91\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R91\">Sulentic &amp; Marziani 1999</a>; <a name=\"InR19\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R19\">Denney 2012</a>). </p>\n<p>We did not find evidence for microlensing effects on the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line cores. However, the cores of the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>], C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] emission lines were clearly affected by microlensing. We obtained a microlensing magnification ratio (B relative to A) <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub> = 1.15</span> (0.15 mag) for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line core, as well as higher ratios of 1.3&#8211;1.4 (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.3&#8211;0.4 mag) for the high-ionization line cores (Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>). In addition, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub> = 1.6</span> (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.5 mag) and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub> = 2</span> (0.75 mag) for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>broad-line emissions, respectively. This last ratio is about 1/3 of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub> ~ 5.7</span> (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1.9 mag) for the nuclear continuum at 5000 &#197;. Therefore, if the size of the NCER is comparable to the Einstein radius of the microlensing objects (stars; e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>), the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>BLER should be more extended (but not much more) than the NCER. When comparing the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line shape in A and B, we also detected a microlensing-induced distortion. Although the two blue wings coincide well with each other, there is a strong enhancement in the red wing of B relative to that of A. This type of distortion suggests that the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>BLER has an anisotropic structure (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R81\">Schneider &amp; Wambsganss 1990</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R1\">Abajas et al. 2002</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R47\">Lewis &amp; Ibata 2004</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R89\">Sluse et al. 2012</a>). For the nuclear continuum, <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#956;</i><sub>BA</sub> ~ 5&#8211;6</span> at 4000&#8211;7000 &#197;, with larger ratios at shorter wavelengths. All these spectral results (together with other time-domain results; see below) confirm that <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> is an uncommon microlensing factory. </p>\n<p>We also conducted a monitoring campaign on <a href=\"http://cdsweb.u-strasbg.fr/cgi-bin/bibobj?2016A%26A...596A..77G&amp;SDSS+J1339+1310\" target=\"_blank\">SDSS J1339+1310</a> in 2009, 2012&#8211;2015 and early 2016 with the LT in the SDSS <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> passband. Hence, this campaign spans five observing seasons and one additional month in 2016. The <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band light curves of the lensed quasar are characterised by typical photometric accuracies of 1.7% (A) and 1.4% (B), and show parallel <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>V</i></span>-shaped variations of A and B. Besides these prominent dips in both light curves, we also detected significant microlensing variability on different timescales, including the presence of microlensing fluctuations lasting 50&#8211;100 d. This extrinsic variability should be taken into account to obtain an unbiased measurement of the time delay between images (e.g. <a name=\"InR31\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R31\">Goicoechea et al. 1998</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R35\">Hainline et al. 2013</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R94\">Tewes et al. 2013a</a>). First, considering only the seasonal (long-timescale) microlensing fluctuation over the period 2009&#8211;2015, we obtained a time delay of 47<span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq126.png\"></span> d (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence interval; A is leading). Second, the estimation of the delay error turned out to be difficult for a more detailed (realistic) microlensing model. However, using a reasonable prior on the delay distribution from simulated light curves (we exclusively focused on solutions in the interval 30&#8211;60 d), we found a 47-d value with <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>10% precision. This broader range of delays practically coincides with the range of plausible solutions from the observed light curves and the realistic microlensing model, and we adopted 47<span class=\"img-inline\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-eq5.png\"></span> d as our final 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> estimation. We also note that the observed interval overlaps with the delay interval predicted by lens models (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>). Finally, the time delay and the microlensing effects we report here are useful tools for doing several types of astrophysical studies (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R83\">Schneider et al. 2006</a>). </p>\n    <hr width=\"30%\" align=\"left\">\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN4\"></a><sup>1</sup> <p class=\"ligne\"> IRAF is distributed by the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy (AURA) under cooperative agreement with the National Science Foundation. This software is available at <a href=\"http://iraf.noao.edu/\" target=\"_blank\">http://iraf.noao.edu/</a></p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN5\"></a><sup>2</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n target=\"_blank\">http://grupos.unican.es/glendama/q1339.htm</a></p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN7\"></a><sup>4</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n          <a href=\"http://classic.sdss.org/dr7/algorithms/spectemplates/spDR2-023.gif\" target=\"_blank\">http://classic.sdss.org/dr7/algorithms/spectemplates/spDR2-023.gif</a>\n href=\"http://grupos.unican.es/glendama/database/\" target=\"_blank\">http://grupos.unican.es/glendama/database/</a> (<a led to similar results.</p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN10\"></a><sup>7</sup> <p class=\"ligne\"> PyCS is distributed by the COSMOGRAIL project, and it is available at <a class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"ack\"></a>Acknowledgments</h2>\n<p>We wish to thank Malte Tewes for his support while using the PyCS software. We also thank the anonymous referee for her/his constructive criticism, which allowed us to improve the original manuscript. This article is also based on observations made with the Liverpool Telescope (LT), operated on the island of La Palma by Liverpool John Moores University in the Spanish Observatorio del Roque de los Muchachos of the Instituto de Astrof&#237;sica de Canarias with financial support from the UK Science and Technology Facilities Council. We thank the staff of both telescopes for a kind interaction before, during and after the observations. We also used data taken from the SDSS databases, and we are grateful to the SDSS collaboration for doing those public databases. J., Netzer, H., &amp; Wills, D. 1985, ApJ, 288, 94\n\n                \n                                                            <a target=\"link_edps\" href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/#abs/1985ApJ...288...94W/abstract\">\n                                                    [NASA ADS]\n                                                </a>\n                                                                                <a target=\"link_edps\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.1086/162767\">\n                                                    [CrossRef]\n                                                </a>\n                                    \n                \n                                    <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?author=Wills%2C+B.+J.%2C+Netzer%2C+H.%2C+%26amp%3B+Wills%2C+D.&journal=ApJ&volume=288&pages=94&publication_year=1985&issn=0004-637X%2C1538-4357\">[Google&nbsp;Scholar]</a>\n                \n                \n                 <span class=\"Z3988\" title=\"\">\n                                    </span>\n\n                \n                            </li>\n                    <li>\n                <a name=\"R103\"></a>Wo\u017aniak, P. R., Alard, C., Udalski, A., et al. 2000, ApJ, 529, 88\n\n                \n                                                            <a target=\"link_edps\" href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/#abs/2000ApJ...529...88W/abstract\">\n                                                    [NASA ADS]\n                                                </a>\n                                                                                <a target=\"link_edps\" href=\"https://doi.org/10.1086/308258\">\n                                                    [CrossRef]\n                                                </a>\n                                    \n                \n                                    <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?author=Wozniak%2C+P.+R.%2C+Alard%2C+C.%2C+Udalski%2C+A.&journal=ApJ&volume=529&issue=1&pages=88&publication_year=2000&issn=0004-637X%2C1538-4357\">[Google&nbsp;Scholar]</a>\n                \n                \n                 <span class=\"Z3988\" title=\"\">\n                                    </span>\n\n                \n                            </li>\n                    <li>\n                <a name=\"R104\"></a>Wucknitz, O., Wisotzki, L., L\u00f3pez, S., &amp; Gregg, M. D. 2003, A&amp;A, 405, 445\n\n                \n                                                            <a target=\"link_edps\"                                                                            <a target=\"link_edps\" href=\"https://www.aanda.org/articles/aa/abs/2003/26/aah4277/aah4277.html\">\n href=\"https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?author=Wucknitz%2C+O.%2C+Wisotzki%2C+L.%2C+Lopez%2C+S.%2C+%26amp%3B+Gregg%2C+M.+D.&journal=A%26A&volume=405&issue=2&pages=445&publication_year=2003&issn=0004-6361%2C1432-0746\">[Google&nbsp;Scholar]</a>\n                \n                \n                 <span class=\"Z3988\" title=\"\">\n                                    </span>\n\n                \n                            </li>\n                </ol>\n    </div>\n\n    \n      <h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"app\"></a>Appendix A:  Spectral extraction biases  and evolution of quasar spectra</h2>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/T1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table A.1</span></a><p>Spectral-to-photometric flux ratios in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band.</p>\n</div></div>\n        </p>\n<p>In Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S2\">2</a>, we present spectra for the two quasar images (A and B) and the lensing galaxy G at two epochs almost separated by the time delay (see Sects. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S1\">1</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S8\">5</a>). Here, we check possible biases introduced by the spectral extraction, and discuss the short-term evolution of the quasar spectra. </p>\n<p>To test the quality of the extraction technique and flux calibration, we compared quasar spectral fluxes averaged over the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> passband (using its response function) and quasar fluxes from <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band frames taken on nights close to those where we carried out spectroscopy with GTC-OSIRIS. These frames belong to our photometric monitoring programme with the LT (see Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S7\">4</a>). For the first spectroscopy epoch (27 March 2014), we used photometric data from LT exposures on 19 March 2014. This night the seeing value was <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1.&#779;5, while the seeing conditions were clearly worse on the closest monitoring night (26 March 2014). For the second spectroscopy epoch (20 May 2014), we compared the spectral fluxes with photometric data on 19 May 2014. The spectral-to-photometric flux ratios in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band are given in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T1\">A.1</a>. Taking the photometric error bars into account (magnitude errors), all ratios are consistent with 1. This is in good agreement with weak DAR-induced distorsions at <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5500&#8211;7000 &#197; (see the second paragraph in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S2\">2</a>), as well as accurate extraction and calibration procedures. In particular, there is no evidence supporting the existence of cross-contamination (crosstalk) between the galaxy and the quasar images, since such spectral extraction bias would produce ratios above or below one. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F15\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F15.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig15_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F15.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. A.1</span></a><p>Spectral ratios. The numbers 1 and 2 denote the first and second spectroscopy epoch. The <i>top and middle panels </i>describe the ratios for the two sources that do not show evidence of variability between both epochs (G and A). The <i>bottom panel </i>describes the ratio between the two quasar images at the same emission time: <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>1 (black) and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>2 (grey).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=15&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"F16\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F16.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig16_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F16.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. A.2</span></a><p>GTC-OSIRIS-R500R spectrum of the lensing galaxy and the early-type galaxy template from the SDSS database. Unlike Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F2\">2</a>, here it is included the red edge of the red grism.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=16&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>The galaxy is a non-variable faint source, and one really sees a reasonable agreement between the bright and light green lines in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F2\">2</a>. In the top panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F15\">A.1</a>, we also show a noisy signal around one that corresponds to the ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>G</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>G</i></span>1 in the overlapping spectral region (4850&#8211;7200 &#197;). Additionally, the LT frames indicated that the image A has the same <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span>-band brightness (19.29 mag) at both epochs. Besides this photometric constancy, in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a>, it is evident that the spectra of A at the two epochs are almost identical between 4850 and 7200 &#197;. The spectral ratio <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>1 confirms the lack of significant variability (see the middle panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F15\">A.1</a>), since its averaged deviation from one is only 2.5%, which does not exceed the typical uncertainty in <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>1 in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band of 2.8% as estimated from the spectral-to-photometric flux ratios in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T1\">A.1</a>. Therefore, one can use the second spectrum of A instead the first when estimating delay-corrected flux ratios <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i>/<i>A</i></span>. This approach has a key advantage over the direct one (based on a comparison between B at the epoch 2 and A at the epoch 1): it provides plausible information over a broader range of wavelengths (3600&#8211;7200 &#197;), including the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>], C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] emission lines. </p>\n<p>Unlike G and A, the B image substantially varies on a timescale of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>50 d (see the bright and light blue lines in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a>). However, in the absence of dust extinction and chromatic microlensing, the spectral ratios <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>1 and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i></span>2/<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>A</i></span>2 must be practically constant for all wavelengths. In the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F15\">A.1</a>, these ratios unambiguously prove the existence of differential extinction-microlensing effects. We remark that the measured signal (incorporating strong dips in the emission line regions) is a fair indicator of the presence of microlensing. </p>\n<p>It is also worth mentioning that the red grism data allow us to estimate the flux ratios for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line cores at the first epoch. The average variation of the flux of A in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band on a timescale of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>50 d is only a few percent (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F10\">10</a>). Hence, typical intrinsic variations of line cores on this timescale are expected to be less than or similar to 1%, and consequently, smaller than the relative uncertainties in the line-core flux ratios from the red grism data (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>5&#8211;10%; see Appendix <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#APP2\">B</a>). In other words, the single-epoch ratios for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] and Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>cores are most likely unbiased by intrinsic variability. In addition, despite the global parallelism between the spectra of G and the early-type galaxy template in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F2\">2</a>, there is a relative drop in the flux of G at <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>9000 &#197; (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F16\">A.2</a>). Although this feature could be true, it may be also an artefact. However, even if G is progressively contaminating the spectrum of B at the reddest wavelengths, the contamination by galaxy light would play the role of an additive pseudo-continuum and would not seriously affect the estimation of flux ratios for the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line. </p>\n      <h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"app\"></a>Appendix B:  Line-core flux ratios</h2>\n<p>First, we used a single power law to fit the nuclear continuum in the blue grism spectra of A and B at rest-frame wavelengths <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8804;</span>2200 &#197;. According to <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R99\">Vanden Berk et al. (2001)</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R45\">Kuraszkiewicz et al. (2002)</a>, we took four pure (nuclear) continuum windows to perform the two fits: C1 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> 4160&#8211;4180 &#197;, C2 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> 4690&#8211;4710 &#197;, C3 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> 5470&#8211;5490 &#197;, and C4 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> 6460&#8211;6480 &#197; (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F17\">B.1</a>). These windows are around 1290 &#197; (C1), 1455 &#197; (C2), 1695 &#197; (C3) and 2000 &#197; (C4) in the quasar rest frame. However, the total continuum under some line cores may differ from the power-law one. Even though the Balmer continuum does not significantly contaminate the quasar spectra at rest-frame wavelengths <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8804;</span>2200 &#197;, the Fe pseudo-continuum (iron forest) plays a role, and this is not taken into account through a global power-law fit (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R101\">Vestergaard &amp; Wilkes 2001</a>). </p>\n<p>For the blue grism spectra, in a second step, we considered the contamination of its four line cores by iron emission (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R99\">Vanden Berk et al. 2001</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R101\">Vestergaard &amp; Wilkes 2001</a>):</p>\n<p>\n          <ul style=\"list-style-type: none;\">\n            <li>\n              <i>Ly</i><span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>: \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> we assumed that the Fe contribution to the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> line profile is negligibly small. However, the blue side of this emission line is noticeably absorbed by clouds of neutral hydrogen (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F17\">B.1</a>) and it is not considered here. We exclusively focused on the red side of the line core. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <i>Si&#8201;</i><span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span><i>/O&#8201;</i><span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>]: \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> the red side of the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] line core is not contaminated by the iron forest. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <i>C&#8201;</i><span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>: \n              <p class=\"ligne\"> the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line core is basically uncontaminated by Fe emission. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <i>C&#8201;</i><span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]: \n              <p class=\"ligne\">\n                the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line lies above an iron (Fe&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>) complex. Hence, in this study, we did not use the global power-law fits to estimate the continuum under the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line core. Instead we obtained local polynomial-law fits, defining 100 &#197; wide continuum windows close to the red and blue side of the emission line. \n              </p>\n            </li>\n          </ul>\n        </p>\n<p>Third, in analysing the red grism spectra, we also used local polynomial-law fits to determine the total continuum under the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line core. The C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line is located on the blue edge of the red grism (see the bright red and blue lines in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F1\">1</a>), and it was not further considered. The Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line at rest-frame wavelengths <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>2800 &#197; lies above the 3000 &#197; bump, which is a very broad feature from <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>2200 to 4000 &#197; (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R99\">Vanden Berk et al. 2001</a>). This mostly consists of blends of Fe&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line emission and Balmer continuum emission (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R102\">Wills et al. 1985</a>). Here, we carried out local linear fits to minimise the Fe&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>-Balmer contamination. Unfortunately, the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>emission line appears close to the red edge of the red grism, so we cannot define continuum windows to the right of its red side. Details on each emission line core, as well as its corresponding flux ratio, are given in the following four paragraphs. </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"F17\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F17.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig17_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F17.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.1</span></a><p>Power-law fits to the nuclear continuum in the blue grism spectra of the quasar. The red and blue circles represent the spectral profiles of A and B, respectively. We show the three pure continuum windows close to the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>, Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>lines (grey highlighted regions), together with the fitted power laws (black lines).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=17&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F18\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F18.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig18_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F18.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.2</span></a><p>Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> emission line profiles. The red and blue lines describe the profiles for the images A and B, respectively. We also highlight the pure continuum window closest to the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> emission (grey) and the red side of the line core, i.e. the 15 &#197; wide region to the right of the main Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> peak (cyan).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=18&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F19\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F19.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig19_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F19.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.3</span></a><p>Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission line profiles. The red and blue lines trace the profiles for the images A and B, respectively. The grey highlighted regions represent some pure continuum windows (see main text), whereas the red side of the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] line core (<i>top panel</i>) and the whole core of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line <i>(bottom panel</i>) are marked in cyan.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=19&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T2\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/T2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table B.1</span></a><p>Line-core flux ratios.</p>\n</div></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"F20\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F20.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig20_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F20.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.4</span></a><p>C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] emission line profiles from the blue and red grism spectra. The red and blue lines describe the profiles for the images A and B, respectively. The grey rectangles are associated with total continuum regions covering 100 &#197;, and the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line core is marked in cyan.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=20&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F21\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F21.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig21_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F21.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.5</span></a><p>Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span> emission line profiles. The red and blue lines describe the profiles for the images A and B, respectively. We highlight three 30 &#197; wide regions: two (total) continuum windows to the left of the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line (grey) and the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line core (cyan).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=21&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"bold\">\n<a name=\"S13\"></a>Appendix B.1: Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span>\n</h3>\n<p>After subtracting the power-law curves that best fit the nuclear continuum of A and B, we derived the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> line profiles in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F18\">B.2</a>. The grey rectangle at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i> ~ 4170</span> &#197; denotes one out of the four pure continuum windows that we used (C1), whereas the cyan highlighted region indicates the red side of the line core (see above). The line core has a width of 30 &#197; (see <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>), but its red side is only 15 &#197; wide (3930&#8211;3945 &#197;). We found a first flux ratio <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>red&#8722;core</sub> = 0.294</span>. The red core in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F18\">B.2</a> was then slightly shifted towards the interval 3935&#8211;3950 &#197; (to try to avoid possible absorption by neutral hydrogen in the vicinity of the BLER and the NLER), leading to a second estimate (B/A)<span class=\"simple-math\"><sub>red&#8722;core</sub> = 0.312</span>. After accounting for the signal noise (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>noise</sub> = 0.003</span>), the error in the power-law fits to the continuum of A and B (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>cont</sub> = 0.001</span>), and the uncertainty in the choice of the red core (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>red</sub> = 0.009</span>), our final 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> measurement was (B/A)<span class=\"simple-math\"><sub>core</sub> = 0.303 &#177; 0.010</span> (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). The flux ratio for the red core was reasonably assumed as a reliable tracer of the flux ratio for the whole core, and the three uncertainties were added in quadrature. In Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F18\">B.2</a>, we can appreciate the presence of N&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">v </span>emission at about 4000 &#197;. This increases the fluxes of the Ly<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i></span> red core in <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>2% (B) and 1% (A), and the two increments partially cancel each other when computing the flux ratio. More specifically, our estimate of B/A only deviates by <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>1% from the uncontaminated flux ratio, so the expected deviation of <span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>0.003 is well below the adopted uncertainty and is irrelevant in subsequent analyses. </p>\n<h3 class=\"bold\">\n<a name=\"S14\"></a>Appendix B.2: Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>\n</h3>\n<p>We used the global power-law fits to find the continuum levels in the spectral regions of the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] and C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emissions, and then obtained the corresponding continuum-subtracted profiles. For the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] line, its profiles appear in the top panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F19\">B.3</a>, where the two grey highlighted regions indicate the pure continuum windows C1 and C2, and the red side of the line core (4525&#8211;4540 &#197;) is marked in cyan. Taking the signal noise and the error in the power-law fits into account, we measured (B/A)<span class=\"simple-math\"><sub>core</sub> = (B/A)<sub>red&#8722;core</sub> = 0.356  &#177;  0.019</span> (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). The final 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span>  uncertainty is dominated by <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>noise</sub> = 0.017</span>. In the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F19\">B.3</a>, we show the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>line profiles, remarking the core of the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>emission at 4990&#8211;5020 &#197; with cyan colour. The pure continuum windows C2 and C3 are also marked with two grey rectangles. The flux ratio for this core was 0.365 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177;</span> 0.007 (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). As usual, we added in quadrature the uncertainties coming from the signal noise (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>noise</sub> = 0.006</span>) and the continuum subtraction (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>cont</sub> = 0.004</span>). </p>\n<h3 class=\"bold\">\n<a name=\"S15\"></a>Appendix B.3: C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>]</h3>\n<p>C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] emission is apparent in the spectra from both grisms. Therefore, we measured two independent flux ratios for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] core. For each pair of spectra (grism), in the region of this emission line, we subtracted linear and quadratic interpolations of the continuum of A and B. These interpolations were based on fluxes within total continuum regions defined by two 100 &#197; wide windows, one to the left of the line and the other to its right. We set the continuum regions CC1 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> [5800&#8211;5900 &#197;, 6400&#8211;6500 &#197;], CC2 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> [5750&#8211;5850 &#197;, 6450&#8211;6550 &#197;] and CC3 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#8801;</span> [5830&#8211;5930 &#197;, 6350&#8211;6450 &#197;]. In the top panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F20\">B.4</a>, we display line profiles from the blue grism spectra, using the CC1 region and a linear fit. Line profiles from the red grism spectra (using the CC2 region and a quadratic fit) are also depicted in the bottom panel of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F20\">B.4</a>. The total continuum windows and the line core at 6150&#8211;6180 &#197; are marked in grey and cyan, respectively. Considering the six possible combinations between continuum region and continuum shape, and comparing the results, we obtained the 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> intervals <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>core</sub> = 0.282 &#177; 0.009</span> (blue grism) and <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>core</sub> = 0.260 &#177; 0.013</span> (red grism).  </p>\n<p>We then combined these two intervals to yield a final measurement <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>core</sub> = 0.275 &#177; 0.007</span>  (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). A flux ratio to the 2&#8211;3% is achieved for the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] core, and the new uncertainty is about four times lower than the error quoted in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R85\">Paper I</a>. </p>\n<h3 class=\"bold\">\n<a name=\"S16\"></a>Appendix B.4: Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span>\n</h3>\n<p>In order to analyse the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>emission line, we took two total continuum windows at 7205&#8211;7235 &#197; and 8590&#8211;8620 &#197;, and then performed linear fits. These fits were used to remove the continuum levels in the region of the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line, and the associated line profiles are shown in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F21\">B.5</a>. In this Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F21\">B.5</a>, the first two grey rectangles denote the two continuum windows, whereas the region in cyan is highlighting the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>core at 9032&#8211;9062 &#197;. Adding in quadrature the uncertainties coming from the signal noise (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>noise</sub></span> = 0.005) and the continuum subtraction (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i><sub>cont</sub></span> = 0.018), we inferred the 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> interval <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>core</sub> = 0.234 &#177; 0.019</span> (see Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>). </p>\n<p>Apart from the new and previous 1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> measurements of the line-core flux ratios, Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a> also provides the new magnitude differences <span class=\"simple-math\">&#916;<i>m</i><sub>core</sub> = 2.5log&#8201;(<i>B</i>/<i>A</i>)<sub>core</sub></span> and their errors (fifth column), as well as relevant information on the degree of ionization of the emitting gas (sixth column; e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R44\">Krolik et al. 1991</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#R27\">Gaskell et al. 2007</a>, and references therein) and the total energy that is required to produce each emitting excited or ionised atom (i.e. <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>E</i><sub>exc</sub></span> or <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>E</i><sub>ion</sub></span> in the seventh column)<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#FN11\">8</a></sup>. For the Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] emission, we only considered the energies for the processes Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">i</span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#8594;</span> Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#8594;</span> Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii </span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#8594;</span> Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>. Taking <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>E</i><sub>ion</sub></span> = 103.7 eV for O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>, this would lead to an average energy of 80.9 eV, which is slightly larger than <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>E</i><sub>ion</sub> ~</span> 60 eV for Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>. The information in the last two columns of Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a> is used in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#S4\">3.1</a>. </p>\n    \n  <h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"tabs\"></a>All Tables</h2>\n<div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/T1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table A.1</span></a><p>Spectral-to-photometric flux ratios in the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i></span> band.</p>\n<div class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T1\">In the text</a></div>\n</div></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/T2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table B.1</span></a><p>Line-core flux ratios.</p>\n<div class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">In the text</a></div>\n</div></div>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"figs\"></a>All Figures</h2>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig1_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 1</span></a><p>GTC-OSIRIS spectra of SDSS J1339+1310ABG in 2014. The red-shifted (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i> = 0.607</span>) spectral template is drawn with magenta lines. Vertical dashed lines are associated with absorption features, while grey highlighted regions are related to atmospheric artefacts.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=2&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F2\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig3_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 3</span></a><p>Line-core magnitude differences for SDSS J1339+1310. Filled circles with error bars describe our measurements in the fifth column of Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#T2\">B.1</a>. Instead of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#955;</i></span> values, the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i></span>-axis displays the wavelength-dependent quantity <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>x</i> &#8733; 1 /<i>&#955;</i></span> (see main text). Assuming microlensing-free fluxes of line cores and Galactic extinction in the lensing galaxy, we also show the best fits using Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>+ C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>data (dotted red line), Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>+ C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] + C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv </span>+ Si&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>/O&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iv</span>] data (dashed red line) and the five measurements (solid red line). For a standard linear extinction in the lensing galaxy, the solid green line traces the best fit to the five magnitude differences.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=3&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F3\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a><p>Multi-component decomposition of carbon line profiles and microlensing of emission lines. The grey rectangles are associated with total continuum regions covering 100 &#197;, and the C&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">iii</span>] line core is marked in cyan.</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=20&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F20\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F21.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16-fig21_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/F21.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. B.5</span></a><p>Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii</span> emission line profiles. The red and blue lines describe the profiles for the images A and B, respectively. We highlight three 30 &#197; wide regions: two (total) continuum windows to the left of the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line (grey) and the Mg&#8201;<span class=\"smallcaps\">ii </span>line core (cyan).</p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\"><a href=\"http://dexter.edpsciences.org/applet.php?pdf_id=21&amp;DOI=10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" target=\"DEXTER\">Open with DEXTER</a></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/12/aa28790-16/aa28790-16.html#F21\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n</div>\n\n\r\n</div>\r\n\r\n    <div id=\"metrics-tabs\" data-doi=\"10.1051/0004-6361/201628790\" data-edps_ref=\"aa28790-16\">\n<nav class=\"toolbar\">\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"metrics-siq\">Current usage metrics</button>\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"info\">About article metrics</button>\n    \n\t\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"return\">Return to article</button>\n</nav>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-siq\">\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-alm\"></div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"info\">\n\t\n\n\t<p>Current usage metrics show cumulative count of Article Views (full-text article views including HTML views, PDF and ePub downloads, according to the available data)  and Abstracts Views on Vision4Press platform.</p>\n\n\t<p>Data correspond to usage on the plateform after 2015.
